<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gordon,_scourged_back,_colored_slide_2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Gordon%2C_scourged_back%2C_colored_slide_2.png/170px-Gordon%2C_scourged_back%2C_colored_slide_2.png" width="170" height="221" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Gordon%2C_scourged_back%2C_colored_slide_2.png/255px-Gordon%2C_scourged_back%2C_colored_slide_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Gordon%2C_scourged_back%2C_colored_slide_2.png/340px-Gordon%2C_scourged_back%2C_colored_slide_2.png 2x" data-file-width="933" data-file-height="1211" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gordon,_scourged_back,_colored_slide_2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ex-slave <a href="/wiki/Gordon_(slave)" title="Gordon (slave)">Gordon</a> displaying <a href="/wiki/Whipping" class="mw-redirect" title="Whipping">whipping</a> scars during a medical examination in 1863 at a <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> military camp in <a href="/wiki/Baton_Rouge" class="mw-redirect" title="Baton Rouge">Baton Rouge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>. Gordon had escaped from slavery on a Louisiana <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantation</a> and gained freedom shortly after reaching the Union camp, later enlisting and serving in the <a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Slavery</b> is, in the strictest sense of the term, any system in which principles of <a href="/wiki/Property_law" title="Property law">property law</a> are applied to people, allowing individuals to own, buy and sell other individuals, as a <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> form of <a href="/wiki/Property" title="Property">property</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> A slave is unable to withdraw unilaterally from such an arrangement and works without <a href="/wiki/Remuneration" title="Remuneration">remuneration</a>. Many scholars now use the term <a href="/wiki/Chattel_slavery" class="mw-redirect" title="Chattel slavery">chattel slavery</a> to refer to this specific sense of legalised, <i>de jure</i> slavery. In a broader sense, however, the word slavery may also refer to any situation in which an individual is <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> forced to work against their own will. Scholars also use the more generic terms such as <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">unfree labour</a> or <b>forced labour</b> to refer to such situations.<sup id="cite_ref-newint_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newint-2">[2]</a></sup> However, and especially under slavery in broader senses of the word, slaves may have some rights and protections according to laws or customs.</p>
<p>Slavery began to exist before written history, in many <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup> A person could become a slave from the time of their birth, capture, or purchase.</p>
<p>While slavery was institutionally recognized by most societies, it has now been <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_slavery_timeline" class="mw-redirect" title="Abolition of slavery timeline">outlawed</a> in all recognized countries,<sup id="cite_ref-Bales2004_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bales2004-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WhiteWhite2014_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WhiteWhite2014-5">[5]</a></sup> the last being <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> in 2007. Nevertheless, there are still more slaves today than at any previous point in history,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> with an estimated 45 million people being in slavery worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The most common form of the slave trade is now commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">human trafficking</a>. Chattel slavery is also <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_21st-century_Islamism#By_ISIS" title="Slavery in 21st-century Islamism">still practiced by the Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a>. In other areas, slavery (or unfree labour) continues through practices such as <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">debt bondage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domestic_worker" title="Domestic worker">domestic servants</a> kept in captivity, certain adoptions in which children are forced to work as slaves, <a href="/wiki/Military_use_of_children" title="Military use of children">child soldiers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-modernslavery_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modernslavery-8">[8]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<p>The English word <i>slave</i> comes from <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>sclave</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> <i>sclavus</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a> σκλάβος, which, in turn, comes from the ethnonym <a href="/wiki/Slav#Ethnonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Slav"><i>Slav</i></a>, because in some early Medieval wars many Slavs were captured and enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> An older interpretation connected it to the Greek verb <i>skyleúo</i> 'to strip a slain enemy'.<sup id="cite_ref-Kluge_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kluge-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a dispute among historians about whether terms such as "unfree labourer" or "enslaved person", rather than "slave", should be used when describing the victims of slavery. According to those proposing a change in terminology, "slave" perpetuates the crime of slavery in language, by reducing its victims to a nonhuman noun instead of, according to Andi Cumbo-Floyd, "carry[ing] them forward as people, not the property that they were". Other historians prefer "slave" because the term is familiar and shorter, or because it accurately reflects the inhumanity of slavery, with "person" implying a degree of autonomy that slavery does not allow for.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chattel_slavery">Chattel slavery</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg/170px-ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg" width="170" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg/255px-ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg/340px-ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1413" data-file-height="1647" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ValuableGangOfYoungNegroes1840.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1840 poster advertising slaves for sale, <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. "Valuable Gang of Young Negroes", 17 men and women, to be sold at auction 25 March 1840 at Banks' Arcade.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Chattel slavery, also called traditional slavery, is so named because people are treated as the <a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">chattel</a> (personal property) of the owner and are bought and sold as commodities. Typically, under the chattel slave system, slave status was imposed on children of the enslaved at birth.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Although it dominated many societies in the past, this form of slavery has been formally abolished and is very rare today. Even when it can be said to survive, it is not upheld by the legal system of any internationally recognized government.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bonded_labour">Bonded labour</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">Debt bondage</a></div>
<p>Indenture, otherwise known as bonded labour or debt bondage, is a form of unfree labour under which a person pledges himself or herself against a loan.<sup id="cite_ref-Bales2004b_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bales2004b-15">[15]</a></sup> The services required to repay the debt, and their duration, may be undefined.<sup id="cite_ref-Bales2004b_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bales2004b-15">[15]</a></sup> Debt bondage can be passed on from generation to generation, with children required to pay off their progenitors' debt.<sup id="cite_ref-Bales2004b_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bales2004b-15">[15]</a></sup> It is the most widespread form of slavery today.<sup id="cite_ref-newint_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newint-2">[2]</a></sup> Debt bondage is most prevalent in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_labour">Forced labour</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">Unfree labour</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">Human trafficking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">Child labour</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Military_use_of_children" title="Military use of children">Military use of children</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinesechildsoldier.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Chinesechildsoldier.jpg/170px-Chinesechildsoldier.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Chinesechildsoldier.jpg/255px-Chinesechildsoldier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Chinesechildsoldier.jpg/340px-Chinesechildsoldier.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2366" data-file-height="2916" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinesechildsoldier.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Kuomintang" title="Kuomintang">Chinese Nationalist</a> soldier, age 10, member of a Chinese division from the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Army_in_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Army in India">X</a>, boarding planes in <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a> bound for China, May 1944.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">Forced labour</a>, or unfree labour, is sometimes used to refer to when an individual is forced to work against their own will, under threat of violence or other punishment<sup id="cite_ref-newint_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newint-2">[2]</a></sup> but the generic term unfree labour is also used to describe chattel slavery, as well as any other situation in which a person is obliged to work against their own will and a person's ability to work productively is under the complete control of another person. This may also include institutions not commonly classified as slavery, such as <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> and <a href="/wiki/Penal_labour" title="Penal labour">penal labour</a>. While some unfree labourers, such as <a href="/wiki/Serf" class="mw-redirect" title="Serf">serfs</a>, have substantive, <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> legal or traditional rights, they also have no ability to terminate the arrangements under which they work, are frequently subject to forms of coercion, such as threats of violence, and experience restrictions on their activities and movement outside their place of work.</p>
<p>Human trafficking primarily involves <a href="/wiki/Woman" title="Woman">women</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child" title="Child">children</a> forced into <a href="/wiki/Prostitution" title="Prostitution">prostitution</a><sup id="cite_ref-voa_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-voa-17">[17]</a></sup> and is the fastest growing form of forced labour,<sup id="cite_ref-newint_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newint-2">[2]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> having been identified as leading hotspots of <a href="/wiki/Commercial_sexual_exploitation_of_children" title="Commercial sexual exploitation of children">commercial sexual exploitation of children</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2007, Human Rights Watch estimated that 200,000 to 300,000 children served as soldiers in current conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forced_marriage">Forced marriage</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">Forced marriage</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Marriage_by_abduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage by abduction">Marriage by abduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">Child marriage</a></div>
<p>A forced marriage may be regarded as a form of slavery by one or more of the parties involved in the marriage, as well as by people observing the marriage. People forced into marriage can be required to engage in sexual activity or to perform domestic duties or other work without any personal control. The customs of <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a> that exist in many parts of the world can lead to buying and selling people into marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Forced marriage continues to be practiced in parts of the world including some parts of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>. Forced marriages may also occur in <a href="/wiki/Immigrant" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigrant">immigrant</a> communities in Europe, the United States, Canada and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marriage_by_abduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage by abduction">Marriage by abduction</a> occurs in many places in the world today, with a national average of 69% of marriages in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> being through abduction.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The International Labour Organization defines child and forced marriage as forms of modern-day slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dependents">Dependents</span></h3>
<p>The word "slave" has also been used to refer to a legal state of dependency to somebody else.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia1_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia1-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In many cases, such as in <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persia</a>, the situations and lives of such slaves could be better than those of other common citizens.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_slavery">Contemporary slavery</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Mauritania" title="Slavery in Mauritania">Slavery in Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_slavery" title="Contemporary slavery">Contemporary slavery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_slavery" title="Child slavery">Child slavery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trafficking_of_children" title="Trafficking of children">Trafficking of children</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration#Slavery" title="Illegal immigration">Illegal immigration §&#160;Slavery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png/220px-Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png/330px-Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png/440px-Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Modern_incidence_of_slavery.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Modern incidence of slavery, as a percentage of the population, by country. Estimates from the <a href="/wiki/Walk_Free_Foundation" title="Walk Free Foundation">Walk Free Foundation</a>. Estimates by other sources may be higher.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Child_labour_Nepal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Child_labour_Nepal.jpg/220px-Child_labour_Nepal.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Child_labour_Nepal.jpg/330px-Child_labour_Nepal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Child_labour_Nepal.jpg/440px-Child_labour_Nepal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Child_labour_Nepal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Child brickyard labourers in Nepal: Thousands of children work as bonded labourers in <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, particularly in the Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Even though slavery is now outlawed in every country,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> the number of slaves today is estimated as between 12 million<sup id="cite_ref-Forced_labour_.E2.80.93_Themes_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forced_labour_.E2.80.93_Themes-33">[33]</a></sup> and 29.8 million.<sup id="cite_ref-WFF_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WFF-34">[34]</a></sup> Several estimates of the number of slaves in the world have been provided.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> According to a broad definition of slavery used by <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Bales" title="Kevin Bales">Kevin Bales</a> of <a href="/wiki/Free_the_Slaves" title="Free the Slaves">Free the Slaves</a> (FTS), an advocacy group linked with <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_International" title="Anti-Slavery International">Anti-Slavery International</a>, there were 27 million people in slavery in 1999, spread all over the world.<sup id="cite_ref-disposable-bales_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-disposable-bales-36">[36]</a></sup> In 2005, the International Labour Organization provided an estimate of 12.3 million forced labourers.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Siddharth_Kara" title="Siddharth Kara">Siddharth Kara</a> has also provided an estimate of 28.4 million slaves at the end of 2006 divided into three categories: <a href="/wiki/Bonded_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Bonded labour">bonded labour</a>/<a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">debt bondage</a> (18.1 million), forced labour (7.6 million), and trafficked slaves (2.7 million).<sup id="cite_ref-Dhaliwal_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhaliwal-38">[38]</a></sup> Kara provides a dynamic model to calculate the number of slaves in the world each year, with an estimated 29.2 million at the end of 2009. According to a 2003 report by <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>, an estimated 15 million children in <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage_in_India" title="Debt bondage in India">debt bondage in India</a> work in slavery-like conditions to pay off their family's debts.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span></h3>
<p>A report by the <a href="/wiki/Walk_Free_Foundation" title="Walk Free Foundation">Walk Free Foundation</a> in 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-Global_Slavery_Index_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Slavery_Index-41">[41]</a></sup> found <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> had the highest number of slaves, nearly 14 million, followed by China (2.9 million), <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (2.1 million), <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>; while the countries with the highest of proportion of slaves were Mauritania, <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>, Pakistan, India and <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2013, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">U.S. State Department</a> released a report on slavery. It placed <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Russia" title="Slavery in Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laogai" title="Laogai">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> in the worst offenders category. <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Sudan" title="Slavery in Sudan">Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> were also at the lowest level. The list also included <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Libya" title="Slavery in Libya">Libya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> among a total of 21 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>
<p>While American slaves in 1809 were sold for around $40,000 (in inflation adjusted dollars), a slave nowadays can be bought for just $90, making replacement more economical than providing long term care.<sup id="cite_ref-Economics_and_Slavery_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Economics_and_Slavery-45">[45]</a></sup> Slavery is a multibillion-dollar industry with estimates of up to $35 billion generated annually.<sup id="cite_ref-investvine_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-investvine-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trafficking">Trafficking</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_trafficking" title="Human trafficking">Human trafficking</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg/220px-Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg/330px-Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg/440px-Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2402" data-file-height="1502" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map3.3Trafficking_compressed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A world map showing countries by prevalence of female trafficking</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Trafficking in human beings (also called human trafficking) is one method of obtaining slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-time_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-47">[47]</a></sup> Victims are typically recruited through deceit or trickery (such as a false job offer, false migration offer, or false marriage offer), sale by family members, recruitment by former slaves, or outright abduction. Victims are forced into a "debt slavery" situation by coercion, deception, fraud, intimidation, isolation, threat, physical force, debt bondage or even <a href="/wiki/Force-feeding" title="Force-feeding">force-feeding</a> with <a href="/wiki/Drug_abuse" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug abuse">drugs of abuse</a> to control their victims.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> "Annually, according to U.S. government-sponsored research completed in 2006, approximately 800,000 people are trafficked across national borders, which does not include millions trafficked within their own countries. Approximately 80 percent of transnational victims are women and girls, and up to 50 percent are minors, reports the U.S. State Department in a 2008 study.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the majority of trafficking victims are women, and sometimes children, who are <a href="/wiki/Forced_prostitution" title="Forced prostitution">forced into prostitution</a> (in which case the practice is called sex trafficking), victims also include men, women and children who are forced into <a href="/wiki/Manual_labour" title="Manual labour">manual labour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state._gov_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state._gov-50">[50]</a></sup> Due to the illegal nature of human trafficking, its exact extent is unknown. A U.S. government report published in 2005, estimates that 600,000 to 800,000 people worldwide are trafficked across borders each year. This figure does not include those who are trafficked internally.<sup id="cite_ref-state._gov_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state._gov-50">[50]</a></sup> Another research effort revealed that between 1.5 million and 1.8 million individuals are trafficked either internally or internationally each year, 500,000 to 600,000 of whom are sex trafficking victims.<sup id="cite_ref-Dhaliwal_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhaliwal-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span></h3>
<p>Examples of slavery in modern times are numerous. For example, <a href="/wiki/Child_slavery" title="Child slavery">Child slavery</a> has commonly been used in the production of <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span></h4>
<p>In 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepalese</a> government abolished the <a href="/wiki/Haliya" title="Haliya">Haliya</a> system, under which 20,000 people were forced to provide free farm labour.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though slavery was officially abolished in <a href="/wiki/Qing_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing China">Qing China</a> in 1910,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> the practice continues unofficially in some regions of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-acme_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acme-55">[55]</a></sup> In June and July 2007, <a href="/wiki/2007_Chinese_slave_scandal" title="2007 Chinese slave scandal">550 people who had been enslaved by brick manufacturers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> were freed by the Chinese government.<sup id="cite_ref-brickkiln_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brickkiln-56">[56]</a></sup> Among those rescued were 69 children.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In response, the Chinese government assembled a force of 35,000 police to check northern Chinese brick kilns for slaves, sent dozens of kiln supervisors to prison, punished 95 officials in Shanxi province for dereliction of duty, and sentenced one kiln foreman to death for killing an enslaved worker.<sup id="cite_ref-brickkiln_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brickkiln-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korean</a> government<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> operates six large <a href="/wiki/Prison_camps_in_North_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Prison camps in North Korea">political prison camps</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> where political prisoners and their families (around 200,000 people)<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> in lifelong detention<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> are subjected to hard slave labour,<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> torture and inhumane treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 2006, the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a> announced it will be seeking "to prosecute members of the ruling <a href="/wiki/State_Peace_and_Development_Council" title="State Peace and Development Council">Myanmar junta</a> for crimes against humanity" over the continuous <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labour" title="Unfree labour">unfree labour</a> of its citizens by the military at the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labor Organization</a> (ILO), an estimated 800,000 people are subject to forced labour in <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Myanmar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_America_and_Caribbean">South America and Caribbean</span></h4>
<p>In 2008, in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> about 5,000&#160;slaves were rescued by government authorities as part of an initiative to eradicate slavery, which was reported as ongoing in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Poverty has forced at least 225,000 <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haitian</a> children to work as <a href="/wiki/Restavec" class="mw-redirect" title="Restavec">restavecs</a> (unpaid household servants); the United Nations considers this to be a form of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East">Middle East</span></h4>
<p>Some tribal sheiks in <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> still keep <a href="/wiki/Afro-Arab" title="Afro-Arab">blacks</a>, called <i>Abd</i>, which means servant or slave in Arabic, as slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to media reports from late 2014 the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a> (ISIL) was selling <a href="/wiki/Yazidis" title="Yazidis">Yazidi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> women as slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> According to Haleh Esfandiari of the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson_International_Center_for_Scholars" title="Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars">Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars</a>, after ISIL militants have captured an area "[t]hey usually take the older women to a makeshift slave market and try to sell them."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> In mid-October 2014, the UN estimated that 5,000 to 7,000 Yazidi women and children were abducted by ISIL and sold into slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In the digital magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Dabiq_(magazine)" title="Dabiq (magazine)">Dabiq</a></i>, ISIL claimed <a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_slavery" title="Islamic views on slavery">religious justification</a> for enslaving Yazidi women whom they consider to be from a heretical sect. ISIL claimed that the Yazidi are idol worshipers and their enslavement is part of the old <a href="/wiki/Shariah" class="mw-redirect" title="Shariah">shariah</a> practice of <a href="/wiki/Ma_malakat_aymanukum" title="Ma malakat aymanukum">spoils of war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall_Street_Journal" title="The Wall Street Journal">The Wall Street Journal</a></i>, ISIL appeals to <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">apocalyptic beliefs</a> and claims "justification by a Hadith that they interpret as portraying the revival of slavery as a precursor to the end of the world".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_contemporary_Africa" title="Slavery in contemporary Africa">Slavery in contemporary Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa,_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_(p.12,_February_1859,_XVI)_-_Copy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa%2C_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_%28p.12%2C_February_1859%2C_XVI%29_-_Copy.jpg/220px-Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa%2C_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_%28p.12%2C_February_1859%2C_XVI%29_-_Copy.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa%2C_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_%28p.12%2C_February_1859%2C_XVI%29_-_Copy.jpg/330px-Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa%2C_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_%28p.12%2C_February_1859%2C_XVI%29_-_Copy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa%2C_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_%28p.12%2C_February_1859%2C_XVI%29_-_Copy.jpg/440px-Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa%2C_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_%28p.12%2C_February_1859%2C_XVI%29_-_Copy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="913" data-file-height="585" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning_of_a_Village_in_Africa,_and_Capture_of_its_Inhabitants_(p.12,_February_1859,_XVI)_-_Copy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Burning of a Village in Africa, and Capture of its Inhabitants (p. 12, February 1859, XVI)<sup id="cite_ref-Offering1859_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Offering1859-82">[82]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Mauritania" title="Slavery in Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, the last country to abolish slavery (in 1981),<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> it is estimated that up to 600,000 men, women and children, or 20% of the population, are enslaved with many used as <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">bonded labour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cnnmauritania_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnnmauritania-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_modern_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavery in modern Africa">Slavery in Mauritania</a> was criminalized in August 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> (although slavery as a practice was legally banned in 1981, it was not a crime to own a slave until 2007).<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Although many slaves have escaped or have been freed since 2007, as of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Slavery&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, only one slave-owner had been sentenced to serve time in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>An article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Middle_East_Quarterly" title="Middle East Quarterly">Middle East Quarterly</a></i> in 1999 reported that slavery is endemic in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Estimates of abductions during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sudanese_Civil_War" title="Second Sudanese Civil War">Second Sudanese Civil War</a> range from 14,000 to 200,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Niger" title="Slavery in Niger">Niger</a>, slavery is also a current phenomenon. A Nigerien study has found that more than 800,000 people are enslaved, almost 8% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Niger installed an anti-slavery provision in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In a landmark ruling in 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">ECOWAS</a> Community Court of Justice declared that the Republic of Niger failed to protect Hadijatou Mani Koraou from slavery, and awarded Mani <a href="/wiki/West_African_CFA_franc" title="West African CFA franc">CFA</a> 10,000,000 (approximately <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$20,000</span>) in reparations.<sup id="cite_ref-Duffy_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Pygmies" class="mw-redirect" title="Pygmies">pygmies</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Congo">Republic of Congo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a> belong from birth to <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantus</a> in a system of slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">U.S. State Department</a>, more than 109,000 children were working on <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a> farms alone in <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> in "the worst forms of <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a>" in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the night of 14–15 April 2014, a group of militants attacked the Government Girls Secondary School in <a href="/wiki/Chibok" title="Chibok">Chibok</a>, Nigeria. They broke into the school, pretending to be guards,<sup id="cite_ref-GuardApr19_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GuardApr19-101">[101]</a></sup> telling the girls to get out and come with them.<sup id="cite_ref-TheTimes_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheTimes-102">[102]</a></sup> A large number of students were taken away in trucks, possibly into the <a href="/wiki/Konduga" title="Konduga">Konduga</a> area of the <a href="/wiki/Sambisa_Forest" title="Sambisa Forest">Sambisa Forest</a> where Boko Haram were known to have fortified camps.<sup id="cite_ref-TheTimes_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheTimes-102">[102]</a></sup> Houses in Chibok were also burned down in the incident.<sup id="cite_ref-GuardApr23_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GuardApr23-103">[103]</a></sup> According to police, approximately 276 children were taken in the attack, of whom 53 had escaped as of 2 May.<sup id="cite_ref-AA52_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA52-104">[104]</a></sup> Other reports said that 329 girls were kidnapped, 53 had escaped and 276 were still missing.<sup id="cite_ref-VOA52_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VOA52-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The students have been forced to convert to Islam<sup id="cite_ref-csmonitor1_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csmonitor1-108">[108]</a></sup> and into marriage with members of Boko Haram, with a reputed "<a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a>" of <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_naira" title="Nigerian naira">₦</a>2,000 each (<a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">$</a>12.50/<a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">£</a>7.50).<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TelegApr30_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TelegApr30-110">[110]</a></sup> Many of the students were taken to the neighbouring countries of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, with sightings reported of the students crossing borders with the militants, and sightings of the students by villagers living in the <a href="/wiki/Sambisa_Forest" title="Sambisa Forest">Sambisa Forest</a>, which is considered a refuge for Boko Haram.<sup id="cite_ref-TelegApr30_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TelegApr30-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 5, 2014 a video in which <a href="/wiki/Boko_Haram" title="Boko Haram">Boko Haram</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Abubakar_Shekau" title="Abubakar Shekau">Abubakar Shekau</a> claimed responsibility for the kidnappings emerged. Shekau claimed that "Allah instructed me to sell them...I will carry out his instructions"<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-112">[112]</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_views_on_slavery" title="Islamic views on slavery">[s]lavery is allowed in my religion</a>, and I shall capture people and make them <a href="/wiki/Ma_malakat_aymanukum" title="Ma malakat aymanukum">slaves</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-CNNEssenceTerror_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNEssenceTerror-113">[113]</a></sup> He said the girls should not have been in school and instead should have been married since girls as young as nine are suitable for marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CNNEssenceTerror_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CNNEssenceTerror-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">History of slavery</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mines_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mines_1.jpg/220px-Mines_1.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mines_1.jpg/330px-Mines_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Mines_1.jpg/440px-Mines_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2346" data-file-height="1738" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mines_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slaves working in a mine, Ancient Greece</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Evidence of slavery predates written records, and has existed in many <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup> Slavery is rare among <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> populations because it requires economic surpluses and a high population density to be viable. Thus, although it has existed among unusually resource-rich hunter gatherers, such as the American Indian peoples of the salmon-rich rivers of the Pacific Northwest Coast, slavery became widespread only with the invention of agriculture during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> about 11,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the earliest known records, slavery is treated as an established institution. The <a href="/wiki/Code_of_Hammurabi" title="Code of Hammurabi">Code of Hammurabi</a> (c. 1760&#160;BC), for example, prescribed death for anyone who helped a slave escape or who sheltered a fugitive.<sup id="cite_ref-wsu._edu_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsu._edu-115">[115]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> <a href="/wiki/The_Bible_and_slavery" title="The Bible and slavery">mentions slavery</a> as an established institution.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slavery was known in almost every ancient civilization and society including <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient China">Ancient China</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_India" title="Bronze Age India">Ancient India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">Hebrew kingdoms</a> of the ancient <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_ancient_Levant" title="History of the ancient Levant">Levant</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_pre-Columbian_cultures" title="List of pre-Columbian cultures">pre-Columbian civilizations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup> Such institutions included <a href="/wiki/Debt_bondage" title="Debt bondage">debt-slavery</a>, punishment for crime, the enslavement of <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoners of war</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_abandonment" title="Child abandonment">child abandonment</a>, and the birth of slave children to slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_antiquity">Classical antiquity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Greece" title="Slavery in ancient Greece">Slavery in ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Rome" title="Slavery in ancient Rome">Slavery in ancient Rome</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg/220px-Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg/330px-Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg/440px-Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1752" data-file-height="1168" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roman_collared_slaves_-_Ashmolean_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slaves in chains, relief found at Smyrna (present day <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0zmir" title="İzmir">İzmir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>), 200&#160;AD</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Records of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Greece" title="Slavery in ancient Greece">slavery in Ancient Greece</a> date as far back as <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean Greece</a>. It is certain that <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">Classical Athens</a> had the largest slave population, with as many as 80,000 in the 6th and 5th centuries BC;<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> two- to four-fifths of the population were slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> expanded outward, <a href="/wiki/Roman_slavery" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman slavery">entire populations were enslaved</a>, thus creating an ample supply from all over Europe and the Mediterranean. <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berber_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Berber people">Berbers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Britons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gauls" title="Gauls">Gauls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab people">Arabs</a>, and many more were slaves used not only for labour, but also for amusement (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiators</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_slavery" title="Sexual slavery">sex slaves</a>). This oppression by an elite minority eventually led to <a href="/wiki/Slave_rebellion" title="Slave rebellion">slave revolts</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Roman_Servile_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Servile Wars">Roman Servile Wars</a>); the <a href="/wiki/Third_Servile_War" title="Third Servile War">Third Servile War</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Spartacus" title="Spartacus">Spartacus</a> (a Thracian) being the most famous and bitter.</p>
<p>By the late Republican era, slavery had become a vital economic pillar in the wealth of Rome, as well as a very significant part of Roman society.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> It is estimated that 25% or more of the population of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> was enslaved, although the actual percentage is debated by scholars, and varied from region to region.<sup id="cite_ref-Harper2011_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harper2011-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Slaves represented 15–25% of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Italy</a>'s population,<sup id="cite_ref-Scheidel_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scheidel-122">[122]</a></sup> mostly captives in war,<sup id="cite_ref-Scheidel_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scheidel-122">[122]</a></sup> especially from <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a><sup id="cite_ref-Joshel545560_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joshel545560-123">[123]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>. Estimates of the number of slaves in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> suggest that the majority of slaves were scattered throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Province">provinces</a> outside of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Italy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scheidel_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scheidel-122">[122]</a></sup> Generally, slaves in Italy were indigenous Italians,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> with a minority of foreigners (including both slaves and freedmen) born outside of Italy estimated at 5% of the total in the capital at its peak, where their number was largest. Those from outside of Europe were predominantly of Greek descent, while the Jewish ones never fully assimilated into Roman society, remaining an identifiable minority. These slaves (especially the foreigners) had higher death rates and lower birth rates than natives, and were sometimes even subjected to mass expulsions.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> The average recorded age at death for the slaves of the city of Rome was extraordinarily low: seventeen and a half years (17.2 for males; 17.9 for females).<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_and_Early_Modern_Europe">Medieval and Early Modern Europe</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_medieval_Europe" title="Slavery in medieval Europe">Slavery in medieval Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbary_slave_trade" title="Barbary slave trade">Barbary slave trade</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">Serfdom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg/220px-Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg/330px-Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg/440px-Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1310" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gniezno_Boleslaus_II.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Adalbert_of_Prague" title="Adalbert of Prague">Adalbert of Prague</a> accuses the Jews of the Christian slave trade against <a href="/wiki/Boleslaus_II,_Duke_of_Bohemia" title="Boleslaus II, Duke of Bohemia">Boleslaus II, Duke of Bohemia</a>, relief of <a href="/wiki/Gniezno_Doors" title="Gniezno Doors">Gniezno Doors</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Large-scale trading in slaves was mainly confined to the South and East of <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">early medieval</a> Europe: the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a> were the destinations, while <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> (along with the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tartary" title="Tartary">Tartary</a>) were important sources. <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab people">Arab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radhanite" title="Radhanite">Radhanite</a> <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> merchants were all involved in the slave trade during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages#Early_Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The trade in European slaves reached a peak in the 10th century following the <a href="/wiki/Zanj_Rebellion" title="Zanj Rebellion">Zanj rebellion</a> which dampened the use of African slaves in the Arab world.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spain_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Spain in the Middle Ages">Medieval Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Portugal" title="History of Portugal">Portugal</a> were the scene of almost constant <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> invasion of the predominantly <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> area. Periodic raiding expeditions were sent from <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> to ravage the Iberian Christian kingdoms, bringing back booty and slaves. In a raid against <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> in 1189, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Almohad_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Almohad dynasty">Almohad</a> caliph <a href="/wiki/Yaqub_al-Mansur" class="mw-redirect" title="Yaqub al-Mansur">Yaqub al-Mansur</a> took 3,000 female and child captives, while his governor of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Spain" title="Córdoba, Spain">Córdoba</a>, in a subsequent attack upon <a href="/wiki/Silves_Municipality,_Portugal" class="mw-redirect" title="Silves Municipality, Portugal">Silves</a>, Portugal in 1191, took 3,000 Christian slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> From the 11th to the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North African</a> <a href="/wiki/Barbary_corsairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbary corsairs">Barbary Pirates</a> engaged in <i><a href="/wiki/Ghazw" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghazw">Razzias</a></i>, raids on European coastal towns, to capture <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> slaves to sell at <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade#Slave_markets_and_fairs" title="Arab slave trade">slave markets</a> in places such as <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg/220px-Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg/330px-Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg/440px-Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="461" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reeve_and_Serfs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Depiction of <a href="/wiki/Socage" title="Socage">socage</a> on the royal <a href="/wiki/Demesne" title="Demesne">demesne</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_England" title="Kingdom of England">feudal England</a>, ca. 1310. Socage is an aspect of <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>, not usually included under the term "slavery".</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Britain, slavery continued to be practiced following the fall of Rome and sections of <a href="/wiki/Hywel_the_Good" class="mw-redirect" title="Hywel the Good">Hywel the Good</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Welsh_law" title="Welsh law">laws</a> dealt with slaves in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval Wales">medieval Wales</a>. The trade particularly picked up after the Viking invasions, with major markets at <a href="/wiki/Chester" title="Chester">Chester</a><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> supplied by Danish, Mercian, and Welsh raiding of one another's borderlands. At the time of the <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i>, nearly 10% of the <a href="/wiki/Norman_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman England">English</a> population were slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_medieval_Europe" title="Slavery in medieval Europe">Slavery in early medieval Europe</a> was so common that <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">the Roman Catholic Church</a> repeatedly prohibited it — or at least the export of Christian slaves to non-Christian lands was prohibited at e.g. the Council of Koblenz (922), the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_London_(1102)" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of London (1102)">Council of London (1102)</a> aimed mainly at the sale of English slaves to Ireland<sup id="cite_ref-Thomas_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-138">[138]</a></sup> and having no legal standing), and the Council of Armagh (1171). In 1452, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">Pope Nicholas V</a> issued the <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> <a href="/wiki/Dum_Diversas" title="Dum Diversas">Dum Diversas</a>, granting the kings of Spain and Portugal the right to reduce any "Saracens (antiquated term referring to Muslims), pagans and any other unbelievers" to perpetual slavery, legitimizing the slave trade as a result of war.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The approval of slavery under these conditions was reaffirmed and extended in his <a href="/wiki/Romanus_Pontifex" title="Romanus Pontifex">Romanus Pontifex</a> bull of 1455. However, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a> forbade enslavement of the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native Americans</a> in 1537 in his papal bull <a href="/wiki/Sublimus_Dei" class="mw-redirect" title="Sublimus Dei">Sublimus Dei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Dominican friars who arrived at the Spanish settlement at <a href="/wiki/Santo_Domingo" title="Santo Domingo">Santo Domingo</a> strongly denounced the enslavement of the local Native Americans. Along with other priests, they opposed their treatment as unjust and illegal in an audience with the Spanish king and in the subsequent royal commission.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG/220px-Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG/330px-Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG/440px-Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG 2x" data-file-width="740" data-file-height="576" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Recovery_of_Tartar_captives.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Tatar</a> raiders enslaved more than 1 million Eastern Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Ottoman_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine–Ottoman Wars">Byzantine-Ottoman wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman wars in Europe</a> brought large numbers of slaves into the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Muslim_population" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries by Muslim population">Islamic world</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> To staff its bureaucracy, the Ottoman Empire established a <a href="/wiki/Janissaries" title="Janissaries">janissary system</a> which seized hundreds of thousands of Christian boys through the <a href="/wiki/Dev%C5%9Firme" class="mw-redirect" title="Devşirme">devşirme</a> system. They were well cared for but were legally slaves owned by the government and were not allowed to marry. They were never bought or sold. The Empire gave them significant administrative and military roles. The system began about 1365; there were 135,000 janissaries in 1826, when the system ended.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lepanto_(1571)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Lepanto (1571)">Battle of Lepanto</a>, 12,000 Christian galley slaves were recaptured and freed from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_fleet" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman fleet">Ottoman fleet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Eastern Europe suffered a series of <a href="/wiki/Mongol_and_Tatar_states_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongol and Tatar states in Europe">Tatar invasions</a>, the goal of which was to loot and capture slaves into <i>jasyr</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-slave_trade_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slave_trade-146">[146]</a></sup> Seventy-five Crimean Tatar raids were recorded into <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland–Lithuania</a> between 1474 and 1569.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approximately 10–20% of the rural population of <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Europe</a> consisted of slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Slavery largely disappeared from Western Europe by the later <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The slave trade became illegal in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> in 1102,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> but England went on to become very active in the lucrative Atlantic slave trade from the seventeenth to the early nineteenth century. In Scandinavia, <a href="/wiki/Thrall" title="Thrall">thralldom</a> was abolished in the mid-14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Slavery persisted longer in <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>. Slavery in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> was forbidden in the 15th century; in <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>, slavery was formally abolished in 1588; they were replaced by the second <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Muscovy</a>, slaves were usually classified as <a href="/wiki/Kholop" title="Kholop">kholops</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span></h4>
<p>In the process of the Mongols' invasion of <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> were enslaved by the Mongols. According to Japanese historian Sugiyama Masaaki (杉山正明) and Funada Yoshiyuki (舩田善之), there were also a certain number of Mongolian slaves owned by Han Chinese during the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>. Moreover, there is no evidence that Han Chinese, who were considered people of the bottom of Yuan society by some research, were suffered a particularly cruel abuse. In the early <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> were enslaved by the Manchurian rulers, although some of them found themselves in positions of power and influence in Manchu administrations and owned their own Han Chinese slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_slave_trade">Arab slave trade</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg/170px-Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg" width="170" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg/255px-Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg/340px-Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg 2x" data-file-width="829" data-file-height="910" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_Zadib_Yemen_13th_century_BNF_Paris.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
13th century slave market in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>. Yemen officially abolished slavery in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In early <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> states of the Western Sudan (present-day West Africa), including <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana</a> (750–1076), <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali</a> (1235–1645), <a href="/wiki/Bamana_Empire" title="Bamana Empire">Segou</a> (1712–1861), and <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai</a> (1275–1591), about a third of the population was enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Slaves were purchased or captured on the frontiers of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a> and then imported to the major centres, where there were slave markets from which they were widely distributed.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> In the 9th and 10th centuries, the black <a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">Zanj</a> slaves may have constituted at least a half of the total population of lower <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup> At the same time, many slaves in the region were also imported from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup> Many slaves were taken in the wars with the Christian nations of <a href="#Medieval_Europe">medieval Europe</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa_2">Africa</span></h4>
<p>Slavery was also widespread in Africa, with both internal and external slave trade.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Crimean-Nogai_raids_into_East_Slavic_lands" class="mw-redirect" title="Crimean-Nogai raids into East Slavic lands">Crimean-Nogai raids into East Slavic lands</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sclavi_Tiganesti.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Sclavi_Tiganesti.jpg/170px-Sclavi_Tiganesti.jpg" width="170" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/Sclavi_Tiganesti.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="205" data-file-height="342" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sclavi_Tiganesti.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An 1852 <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachian</a> poster advertising an auction of <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Romania" title="Slavery in Romania">Roma slaves</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bucharest" title="Bucharest">Bucharest</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_White_Slave_statue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/The_White_Slave_statue.jpg/170px-The_White_Slave_statue.jpg" width="170" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/The_White_Slave_statue.jpg/255px-The_White_Slave_statue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/The_White_Slave_statue.jpg/340px-The_White_Slave_statue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="926" data-file-height="1090" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_White_Slave_statue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
«The White Slave» (<a href="/wiki/Abastenia_St._Leger_Eberle" title="Abastenia St. Leger Eberle">Eberle</a>, 1913)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Until the late 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a> (a Muslim Tatar state) maintained a massive slave trade with the Ottoman Empire and the Middle East,<sup id="cite_ref-slave_trade_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slave_trade-146">[146]</a></sup> exporting about 2 million slaves from Poland-Lithuania and Russia over the period 1500–1700.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
