<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> (1939–1945) <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour_under_German_rule_during_World_War_II" title="Forced labour under German rule during World War II">Nazi Germany effectively enslaved about 12 million people</a>, both those considered undesirable and citizens of countries they conquered, with the avowed intention of treating these <a href="/wiki/Untermenschen" class="mw-redirect" title="Untermenschen">untermenschen</a> as a permanent slave class of inferior beings who could be worked until they died but who possessed neither the rights nor the legal status of members of the <a href="/wiki/Aryan_race" title="Aryan race">Aryan race</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Empire">Ottoman Empire</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">Zanj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbary_slave_trade" title="Barbary slave trade">Barbary slave trade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Devshirme" title="Devshirme">Devshirme</a></div>
<p>The Ottoman Empire owned and traded slaves on a massive scale. Many slaves were taken by conquest and the suppression of rebellions, in the aftermath of which, entire populations were sometimes enslaved and sold across the Empire, reducing the risk of future rebellion. The Ottomans also purchased slaves from traders who brought slaves into the Empire from Europe and Africa.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa_3">Africa</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Africa" title="Slavery in Africa">Slavery in Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:African_slave_trade.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/African_slave_trade.png/220px-African_slave_trade.png" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/African_slave_trade.png/330px-African_slave_trade.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/African_slave_trade.png/440px-African_slave_trade.png 2x" data-file-width="721" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:African_slave_trade.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The main routes that were used to transport slaves across medieval Africa.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>, the capital of <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, captured <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europeans</a> were forced into slavery. Raids by <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> on coastal villages and ships extending from Italy to Iceland, enslaved an estimated 1 million to 1¼ million Europeans between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> This eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Bombardment_of_Algiers_(1816)" title="Bombardment of Algiers (1816)">bombardment of Algiers</a> by an Anglo-Dutch fleet in 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg/170px-Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg/255px-Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg/340px-Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2531" data-file-height="3585" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marchands_d%27esclaves_de_Gor%C3%A9e-Jacques_Grasset_de_Saint-Sauveur_mg_8526.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slave traders in <a href="/wiki/Gor%C3%A9e" title="Gorée">Gorée</a>, Senegal, 18th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Half the population of the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto" title="Sokoto">Sokoto</a> caliphate of the 19th century (based in the future northern Nigeria) were slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup> The Swahili-Arab slave trade reached its height about 160 years ago, when, for example, approximately 20,000 slaves were considered to be carried yearly from <a href="/wiki/Nkhotakota" title="Nkhotakota">Nkhotakota</a> on Lake Malawi to Kilwa.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Roughly half the population of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> was enslaved.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_African_History" title="Encyclopedia of African History">Encyclopedia of African History</a></i>, "It is estimated that by the 1890s the largest slave population of the world, about 2 million people, was concentrated in the territories of the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a>. The use of slave labour was extensive, especially in agriculture."<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The Anti-Slavery Society estimated there were 2 million slaves in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in the early 1930s out of an estimated population of 8 to 16 million.<sup id="cite_ref-twentieth1_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-twentieth1-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/220px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/330px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/440px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="602" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arab slave traders and their captives along the Ruvuma river (in today's Tanzania and Mozambique).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hugh_Clapperton" title="Hugh Clapperton">Hugh Clapperton</a> in 1824 believed that half the population of <a href="/wiki/Kano" title="Kano">Kano</a> were enslaved people.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> W. A. Veenhoven wrote: "The German doctor, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Nachtigal" title="Gustav Nachtigal">Gustav Nachtigal</a>, an eye-witness, believed that for every slave who arrived at a market three or four died on the way&#160;... <a href="/wiki/John_Scott_Keltie" title="John Scott Keltie">Keltie</a> (<i>The Partition of Africa</i>, London, 1920) believes that for every slave the Arabs brought to the coast at least six died on the way or during the slavers' raid. <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">Livingstone</a> puts the figure as high as ten to one."<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most famous slave traders on the eastern <a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">Zanj</a> (Bantu) coast was <a href="/wiki/Tippu_Tip" title="Tippu Tip">Tippu Tip</a>, himself the grandson of a slave. The <i>prazeros</i> were slave-traders along the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a>. North of the Zambezi, the <a href="/wiki/WaYao" class="mw-redirect" title="WaYao">waYao</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makua_people" title="Makua people">Makua people</a> played a similar role as professional slave-raiders and -traders. Still further north were the <a href="/wiki/Nyamwezi_people" title="Nyamwezi people">Nyamwezi</a> slave-traders.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_2">Asia</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_Asia" title="History of slavery in Asia">History of slavery in Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/170px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/255px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/340px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="903" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A contract from the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> that records the purchase of a 15-year-old slave for six bolts of plain silk and five <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">coins</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, about one-fifth of the population consisted of slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Britannica_156-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-156">[156]</a></sup> The city was a major centre of the slave trade in the 15th and later centuries. By 1475 most of the slaves were provided by Tatar raids on Slavic villages.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> It has been estimated that some 200,000 slaves—mainly <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>—were imported into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> between 1800 and 1909.<sup id="cite_ref-Abolition_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-175">[175]</a></sup> As late as 1908, women slaves were still sold in the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> A slave market for captured <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> slaves was centred in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Khiva" title="Khanate of Khiva">khanate of Khiva</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> In the early 1840s, the population of the Uzbek states of <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Bukhara" title="Emirate of Bukhara">Bukhara</a> and Khiva included about 900,000 slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Abolition_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-175">[175]</a></sup> Darrel P. Kaiser wrote, "<a href="/wiki/Kazakhs" title="Kazakhs">Kazakh</a>-<a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kirghiz</a> tribesmen kidnapped 1573 settlers from colonies [German settlements in Russia] in 1774 alone and only half were successfully ransomed. The rest were killed or enslaved."<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bartle_Frere" title="Henry Bartle Frere">Henry Bartle Frere</a> (who sat on the Viceroy's Council), there were an estimated 8 or 9 million slaves in <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">India</a> in 1841. About 15% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Malabar_District" title="Malabar District">Malabar</a> were slaves. Slavery was abolished in <a href="/wiki/British_India" class="mw-redirect" title="British India">British India</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_India" title="Slavery in India">Indian Slavery</a> Act V. of 1843.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing Dynasty">Imperial</a> government formally abolished slavery in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in 1906, and the law became effective in 1910.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Social_classes_of_Tibet" class="mw-redirect" title="Social classes of Tibet">Nangzan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibetan</a> history were, according to Chinese sources, hereditary household slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> period of Korea, members of the slave class were known as <i>nobi</i>. The nobi were socially indistinct from freemen other than the ruling <a href="/wiki/Yangban" title="Yangban">yangban</a> class, and some possessed property rights, legal entities and civil rights. Hence, some scholars argue that it's inappropriate to call them "slaves",<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> while some scholars describe them as <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell2004-153_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2004-153-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> The nobi population could fluctuate up to about one-third of the population, but on average the nobi made up about 10% of the total population.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> In 1801, the vast majority of government nobi were emancipated,<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> and by 1858 the nobi population stood at about 1.5 percent of the total population of Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-nobi_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobi-186">[186]</a></sup> The hereditary nobi system was officially abolished around 1886–87 and the rest of the nobi system was abolished with the <a href="/wiki/Gabo_Reform" title="Gabo Reform">Gabo Reform</a> of 1894,<sup id="cite_ref-nobi_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobi-186">[186]</a></sup> but traces remained until 1930.</p>
<p>In late 16th century <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, slavery as such was officially banned, but forms of contract and indentured labour persisted alongside the period penal codes' forced labour.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hill_tribe#South-East_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Hill tribe">hill tribe</a> people in <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> were "hunted incessantly and carried off as slaves by the Siamese (Thai), the Anamites (Vietnamese), and the Cambodians".<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> A Siamese military campaign in Laos in 1876 was described by a British observer as having been "transformed into slave-hunting raids on a large scale".<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The census, taken in 1879, showed that 6% of the population in the <a href="/wiki/Monarchies_of_Malaysia" title="Monarchies of Malaysia">Malay</a> sultanate of <a href="/wiki/Perak" title="Perak">Perak</a> were slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Abolition_175-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-175">[175]</a></sup> Enslaved people made up about two-thirds of the population in part of <a href="/wiki/North_Borneo" title="North Borneo">North Borneo</a> in the 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-Abolition_175-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abolition-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout the 1930s and 1940s the <a href="/wiki/Yi_people" title="Yi people">Yi people</a> (also known as Nuosu) of China terrorized Sichuan to rob and enslave non-Nuosu including <a href="/wiki/Han_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Han people">Han people</a>. The descendants of the Han Chinese slaves are the White Yi (白彝) and they outnumber the Black Yi (黑彝) aristocracy by ten to one.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> As much as tens of thousands of Han slaves were incorporated into Nuosu society every year. The Han slaves and their offspring were used for manual labor.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> There is a saying goes like: "the worst insult to a Nuosu is to call him a "Han" (with the implication being that "your ancestors were slaves")".<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">Encomienda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mita_(Inca)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mita (Inca)">Mita (Inca)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Brazil" title="Slavery in Brazil">Slavery in Brazil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">Slavery in the United States</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slave_ship_diagram.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Slave_ship_diagram.png/230px-Slave_ship_diagram.png" width="230" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Slave_ship_diagram.png/345px-Slave_ship_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Slave_ship_diagram.png/460px-Slave_ship_diagram.png 2x" data-file-width="1001" data-file-height="1441" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slave_ship_diagram.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram showing the general arrangements of a <a href="/wiki/Slave_ship" title="Slave ship">slave ship</a> and the alignment of captive slaves during the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Slavery in the Americas had a contentious history, and played a major role in the history and evolution of some countries, triggering a <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">revolution</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>) and <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">a civil war</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>), as well as numerous rebellions. The <a href="/wiki/Aztec_slavery" title="Aztec slavery">Aztecs</a> had slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Other Amerindians, such as the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca</a> of the Andes, the <a href="/wiki/Tup%C3%AD_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Tupí people">Tupinambá</a> of Brazil, the <a href="/wiki/Muscogee_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscogee people">Creek</a> of Georgia, and the <a href="/wiki/Comanche" title="Comanche">Comanche</a> of Texas, also owned slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The maritime town of <a href="/wiki/Lagos,_Portugal" title="Lagos, Portugal">Lagos</a> was the first slave market created in Portugal (one of the earliest colonizers of the Americas) for the sale of imported African slaves—the <i>Mercado de Escravos</i>, opened in 1444.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodman_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Oliveira_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-195">[195]</a></sup> In 1441, the first slaves were brought to Portugal from northern <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oliveira_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1519, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>'s first <a href="/wiki/Afro-Mexican#African_slavery_in_Mexico" class="mw-redirect" title="Afro-Mexican">Afro-Mexican</a> slave was brought by <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">Hernán Cortés</a>.</p>
<p>By 1552, <a href="/wiki/Black_people" title="Black people">black African</a> slaves made up 10% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> In the second half of the 16th century, the Crown gave up the monopoly on slave trade and the focus of European trade in African slaves shifted from import to Europe to slave transports directly to tropical colonies in the Americas—in the case of Portugal, especially <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oliveira_195-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-195">[195]</a></sup> In the 15th century one-third of the slaves were resold to the African market in exchange of gold.<sup id="cite_ref-Klein2010_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klein2010-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>In order to establish itself as an American empire, Spain had to fight against the relatively powerful civilizations of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> conquest of the indigenous peoples in the Americas included using the Natives as forced labour. The <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Spanish_New_World_colonies" title="Slavery in the Spanish New World colonies">Spanish colonies</a> were the first Europeans to use African slaves in the New World on islands such as <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>.)<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_Las_Casas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bartolomé de Las Casas">Bartolomé de Las Casas</a>, a 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spanish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> historian, participated in campaigns in Cuba (at <a href="/wiki/Bayamo" title="Bayamo">Bayamo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camag%C3%BCey" title="Camagüey">Camagüey</a>) and was present at the massacre of <a href="/wiki/Hatuey" title="Hatuey">Hatuey</a>; his observation of that massacre led him to fight for a social movement away from the use of natives as slaves and towards the importation of African Blacks as slaves. Also, the alarming decline in the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">native</a> population had spurred the first royal laws protecting the native population (<a href="/wiki/Laws_of_Burgos" title="Laws of Burgos">Laws of Burgos, 1512–1513</a>).</p>
<p>The first African slaves arrived in Hispaniola in 1501.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> In 1518, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles I of Spain</a> agreed to ship slaves directly from Africa. England played a prominent role in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>. The "<a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade#Atlantic_triangular_slave_trade" title="Triangular trade">slave triangle</a>" was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> and his associates. In 1640 a Virginia court sentenced <a href="/wiki/John_Punch_(slave)" title="John Punch (slave)">John Punch</a> to slavery, forcing him to serve his master, Hugh Gwyn, for the remainder of his life. This was the first legal sanctioning of slavery in the English colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> In 1655, A black man, <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Johnson_(colonist)" title="Anthony Johnson (colonist)">Anthony Johnson</a> of Virginia, was granted ownership of John Casor as the result of a civil case.<sup id="cite_ref-Billings2009_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billings2009-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Henrietta Marie</i> was probably built in France sometime in the 17th century and carried a crew of about eighteen men. The ship came into English possession late in the 17th century, possibly as a <a href="/wiki/Prize_(law)" title="Prize (law)">war prize</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War" title="Nine Years' War">War of the Grand Alliance</a>. It was put to use in the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>, making at least two voyages carrying Africans to slavery in the West Indies. On its first voyage, in 1697–1698, the ship carried more than 200 people from Africa that were sold as slaves in <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>. In 1699 the <i>Henrietta Marie</i> sailed from England on the first leg of the <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">triangular trade</a> route with a load of trade goods, including iron and copper bars, pewter utensils, glass beads, cloth and brandy. The ship sailed under license from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_African_Company" title="Royal African Company">Royal African Company</a> (which held a monopoly on English trade with Africa), in exchange for ten percent of the profits of the voyage. It is known to have traded for African captives at <a href="/wiki/Kalabari_Kingdom" title="Kalabari Kingdom">New Calabar</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Guinea_Coast" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinea Coast">Guinea Coast</a>. The ship then sailed on the second leg of its voyage, from Africa to the West Indies, and in May 1701 landed 191 Africans for sale in <a href="/wiki/Port_Royal" title="Port Royal">Port Royal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a>. The <i>Henrietta Marie</i> then loaded a cargo of sugar, cotton, <a href="/wiki/Dyewoods" title="Dyewoods">dyewoods</a> (indigo) and ginger to take back to England on the third leg of the triangular route. After leaving Port Royal on 18 May 1701, the ship headed for the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Channel" title="Yucatán Channel">Yucatán Channel</a> to pass around the western end of <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> (thus avoiding the pirates infesting the passage between Cuba and <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">Hispaniola</a>) and catch the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>, the preferred route for all ships leaving the Caribbean to return to Europe. A month later, the <i>Henrietta Marie</i> <a href="/wiki/Shipwreck" title="Shipwreck">wrecked</a> on New Ground Reef near the <a href="/wiki/Marquesas_Keys" title="Marquesas Keys">Marquesas Keys</a>, approximately 35 miles (56 kilometres) west of <a href="/wiki/Key_West,_Florida" title="Key West, Florida">Key West</a>. All aboard were lost.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_(1823),_plate_III_-_BL.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg/220px-Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg/330px-Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg/440px-Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_%281823%29%2C_plate_III_-_BL.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1994" data-file-height="1392" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slaves_working_on_a_plantation_-_Ten_Views_in_the_Island_of_Antigua_(1823),_plate_III_-_BL.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Planting the <a href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" class="mw-redirect" title="Sugar cane">sugar cane</a>, British colony of <a href="/wiki/Antigua" title="Antigua">Antigua</a>, 1823</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pirates often targeted slavers. For example, the 300 ton English <a href="/wiki/Frigate" title="Frigate">frigate</a> <i>Concord</i> launched in 1710 but was captured by the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> one year later. She was modified to hold more cargo, including <a href="/wiki/Slaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Slaves">slaves</a>, and renamed <i>La Concorde de Nantes</i>. Sailing as a <a href="/wiki/Slave_ship" title="Slave ship">slave ship</a>, she was captured by the pirate <a href="/wiki/Captain_(nautical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Captain (nautical)">Captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Hornigold" title="Benjamin Hornigold">Benjamin Hornigold</a> on November 28, 1717, near the island of <a href="/wiki/Martinique" title="Martinique">Martinique</a>. Hornigold turned her over to one of his men, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Teach" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward Teach">Edward Teach</a> (later known as <a href="/wiki/Blackbeard" title="Blackbeard">Blackbeard</a>), and made him her captain. Teach then renamed her the <i><a href="/wiki/Queen_Anne%27s_Revenge" title="Queen Anne's Revenge">Queen Anne's Revenge</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-D._Moore._1997_pp._31_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-D._Moore._1997_pp._31-205">[205]</a></sup> By 1750, slavery was a legal institution in all of the 13 <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">American colonies</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-cornerstones_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cornerstones-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> and the profits of the slave trade and of <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">West Indian</a> plantations amounted to 5% of the <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Economic history of the United Kingdom">British economy</a> at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slavery17.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Slavery17.jpg/220px-Slavery17.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Slavery17.jpg/330px-Slavery17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Slavery17.jpg/440px-Slavery17.jpg 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="531" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slavery17.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Slave-branding</i> by Bourne, George, 1853</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The trans-Atlantic slave trade peaked in the late 18th century, when the largest number of slaves were captured on raiding expeditions into the interior of West Africa. These expeditions were typically carried out by <a href="/wiki/List_of_kingdoms_in_pre-colonial_Africa" title="List of kingdoms in pre-colonial Africa">African kingdoms</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_Empire" title="Ashanti Empire">Ashanti Empire</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dahomey" title="Dahomey">Dahomey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Aro_Confederacy" title="Aro Confederacy">Aro Confederacy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Europeans rarely entered the interior of Africa, due to fierce African resistance. The slaves were brought to coastal outposts where they were traded for goods. A significant portion of <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African Americans</a> in North America are descended from <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-muse.jhu.edu_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muse.jhu.edu-212">[212]</a></sup> Through a series of conflicts, primarily with the <a href="/wiki/Fulani_Jihad_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Fulani Jihad States">Fulani Jihad States</a>, about half of the Senegambian Mandinka were converted to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> while as many as a third were sold into slavery to the Americas through capture in conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-muse.jhu.edu_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muse.jhu.edu-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Slave_Trader,_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Slave_Trader%2C_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg/220px-Slave_Trader%2C_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Slave_Trader%2C_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg/330px-Slave_Trader%2C_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Slave_Trader%2C_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg/440px-Slave_Trader%2C_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Slave_Trader,_Sold_to_Tennessee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A coffle of slaves being driven on foot from <a href="/wiki/Staunton,_Virginia" title="Staunton, Virginia">Staunton</a>, Virginia, to <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a> in 1850</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman-slave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Woman-slave.jpg/170px-Woman-slave.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Woman-slave.jpg/255px-Woman-slave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Woman-slave.jpg/340px-Woman-slave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="492" data-file-height="608" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Woman-slave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mid-19th century portrait of an older <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> woman with her child slave servant</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An estimated 12 million Africans arrived in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> from the 16th to the 19th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> Of these, an estimated 645,000 were brought to what is now the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. The usual estimate is that about 15% of slaves died during the voyage, with mortality rates considerably higher in Africa itself in the process of capturing and transporting indigenous peoples to the ships.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many Europeans who arrived in North America during the 17th and 18th centuries came under contract as indentured servants.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> The transformation from indentured servitude to slavery was a gradual process in Virginia. The earliest legal documentation of such a shift was in 1640 where a negro, <a href="/wiki/John_Punch_(slave)" title="John Punch (slave)">John Punch</a>, was sentenced to lifetime slavery for attempting to run away. This case also marked the disparate treatment of Africans as held by the Virginia County Court, as two white runaways received far lesser sentences.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> After 1640, planters started to ignore the expiration of indentured contracts and kept their servants as slaves for life. This was demonstrated by the case Johnson v. Parker, where the court ruled that <a href="/wiki/John_Casor" title="John Casor">John Casor</a>, an indentured servant, be returned to Johnson who claimed that Casor belonged to him for his life.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/1860_United_States_Census" title="1860 United States Census">1860 U. S. census</a>, 393,975 individuals, representing 8% of all US families, owned 3,950,528 slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> One-third of Southern families owned slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos,_1839.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos%2C_1839.jpg/220px-Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos%2C_1839.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos%2C_1839.jpg/330px-Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos%2C_1839.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos%2C_1839.jpg/440px-Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos%2C_1839.jpg 2x" data-file-width="516" data-file-height="640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jacques_Etienne_Arago_-_Castigo_de_Escravos,_1839.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Slave punishment</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Arago" title="Jacques Arago">Jacques Etienne Arago</a>, Brazil's Museu Afro Brasil, 1839</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The largest number of slaves were shipped to <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> In the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">viceroyalty of New Granada</a>, corresponding mainly to modern <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, the free black population in 1789 was 420,000, whereas African slaves numbered only 20,000. Free blacks also outnumbered slaves in Brazil. By contrast, in <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, free blacks made up only 15% in 1827; and in the French colony of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Domingue" title="Saint-Domingue">Saint-Domingue</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haiti</a>) it was a mere 5% in 1789.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Author Charles Rappleye argued that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In the West Indies in particular, but also in North and South America, slavery was the engine that drove the mercantile empires of Europe. It appeared, in the eighteenth century, as universal and immutable as human nature.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although the trans-Atlantic slave trade ended shortly after the American Revolution, slavery remained a central economic institution in the Southern states of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, from where slavery expanded with the westward movement of population.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Historian Peter Kolchin wrote, "By breaking up existing families and forcing slaves to relocate far from everyone and everything they knew" this migration "replicated (if on a reduced level) many of [the] horrors" of the Atlantic slave trade.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg/220px-Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg/330px-Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg/440px-Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1163" data-file-height="783" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Senhora_escravos_1860.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lady in litter being carried by her slaves, <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">province of São Paulo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, ca.1860.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Ira_Berlin" title="Ira Berlin">Ira Berlin</a> called this forced migration the Second <a href="/wiki/Middle_Passage" title="Middle Passage">Middle Passage</a>. Characterizing it as the "central event" in the life of a slave between the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> and the Civil War, Berlin wrote that whether they were uprooted themselves or simply lived in fear that they or their families would be involuntarily moved, "the massive deportation traumatized black people, both slave and free.."<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg/220px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg/330px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg/440px-Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3467" data-file-height="2620" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tropenmuseum_Royal_Tropical_Institute_Objectnumber_3444-7_Begrafenis_bij_plantageslaven2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Funeral at slave plantation, Suriname. Colored lithograph printed circa 1840–1850, digitally restored.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1860, 500,000 slaves had grown to 4 million. As long as slavery expanded, it remained profitable and powerful and was unlikely to disappear. Although complete statistics are lacking, it is estimated that 1,000,000 slaves moved west from the <a href="/wiki/Old_South" title="Old South">Old South</a> between 1790 and 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-Kolchin_pp._582_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolchin_pp._582-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most of the slaves were moved from <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/The_Carolinas" title="The Carolinas">Carolinas</a>. Michael Tadman, in a 1989 book <i>Speculators and Slaves: Masters, Traders, and Slaves in the Old South</i>, indicates that 60–70% of interregional migrations were the result of the sale of slaves. In 1820, a child in the Upper South had a 30% chance to be sold south by 1860.<sup id="cite_ref-Kolchin_pp._582_227-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kolchin_pp._582-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_immigration_to_Puerto_Rico#Abolition_of_Slavery" class="mw-redirect" title="African immigration to Puerto Rico">African slavery</a> was finally abolished on March 22, 1873.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_East_and_North_Africa">Middle East and North Africa</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Slavery in the Ottoman Empire">Slavery in the Ottoman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_on_the_Barbary_Coast" title="Slavery on the Barbary Coast">Slavery on the Barbary Coast</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg/220px-360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg" width="220" height="265" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg/330px-360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg/440px-360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg 2x" data-file-width="845" data-file-height="1016" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:360Niklas_St%C3%B6r_Entf%C3%BChrung_in_die_Sklaverei.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman wars in Europe</a> resulted in many captive Europeans being carried deep into Ottoman territory.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Robert Davis, between 1 million and 1.25 million <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">Europeans</a> were captured by <a href="/wiki/Barbary_corsairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbary corsairs">Barbary pirates</a> and sold as slaves in <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-davis2003_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davis2003-228">[228]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> There was also an extensive trade in Christian slaves in the Black Sea region for several centuries until the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a> was destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in 1783.<sup id="cite_ref-Crimea_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crimea-230">[230]</a></sup> In the 1570s close to 20,000 slaves a year were being sold in the Crimean port of <a href="/wiki/Feodosiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Feodosiya">Kaffa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> The slaves were captured in southern Russia, <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland-Lithuania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldavia" title="Moldavia">Moldavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallachia" title="Wallachia">Wallachia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Circassia" title="Circassia">Circassia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatar</a> horsemen.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> Some researchers estimate that altogether more than 3 million people were captured and enslaved during the time of the Crimean Khanate.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Persian_slave.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Persian_slave.jpg/220px-Persian_slave.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Persian_slave.jpg/330px-Persian_slave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Persian_slave.jpg/440px-Persian_slave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1925" data-file-height="1273" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Persian_slave.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Persian slave in the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Khiva" title="Khanate of Khiva">Khanate of Khiva</a>, 19th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg/220px-Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg/330px-Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg/440px-Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg 2x" data-file-width="825" data-file-height="621" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Captain_walter_croker_horror_stricken_at_algiers_1815.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
British captain witnessing the miseries of the Christian slaves in Algiers, 1815</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a> lasted more than a millennium.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> As recently as the early 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>'s slave population was estimated at 300,000.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> Along with Yemen, the Saudis abolished slavery only in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-237">[237]</a></sup> Historically, slaves in the <a href="/wiki/Arab_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Arab World">Arab World</a> came from many different regions, including <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> (mainly <i><a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">Zanj</a></i>),<sup id="cite_ref-lewis1992_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lewis1992-238">[238]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> (mainly <a href="/wiki/Tartary" title="Tartary">Tartars</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> (mainly <i><a href="/wiki/Saqaliba" title="Saqaliba">Saqaliba</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Soldier_Khan_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soldier_Khan-240">[240]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Omani</a> Arabs <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a> became <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>'s main slave port, with as many as 50,000 enslaved Africans passing through every year during the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> Some historians estimate that between 11 and 18 million African slaves crossed the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> Desert from 650 to 1900 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-Slavery_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slavery-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eduard_R%C3%BCppell" title="Eduard Rüppell">Eduard Rüppell</a> described the losses of Sudanese slaves being transported on foot to Egypt: "after the Daftardar bey's 1822 campaign in the southern Nuba mountains, nearly 40,000 slaves were captured. However, through bad treatment, disease and desert travel barely 5000 made it to Egypt.."<sup id="cite_ref-campbell2007_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campbell2007-244">[244]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, starting in the 8th century, also raided coastal areas around the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, and became known as the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_corsairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbary corsairs">Barbary pirates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> It is estimated that they captured 1.25 million white slaves from <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> The mortality rate was very high. For instance, plague killed a third to two-thirds of the 30,000 occupants of the slave pens in <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> in 1662.<sup id="cite_ref-davis2003_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-davis2003-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abolitionism">Abolitionism</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">Abolitionism</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_slavery_timeline" class="mw-redirect" title="Abolition of slavery timeline">Abolition of slavery timeline</a></div>
